          "parts": [
            "## CHAPTER 5 — JUNCTION TWELVE  \n**Tide 1 | Striking**  \n**POV: Jhace**\n\nThe Underface didn’t forgive optimism.\n\nIt rewarded repetition. It rewarded ugly caution. It rewarded the kind of man who could stare at a seam for half a Tide and still call it wrong when everyone else called it fine.\n\nJhace stood at Junction Twelve with a tremor still living in his hands like a debt he couldn’t pay off. He kept his fingers curled into his gloves, not because it stopped the shaking, but because it kept the crew from seeing how bad it was.\n\nThe collar sat there like a liar pretending to be a joint.\n\nTiffani’s indicators rested on the stone lip beside his slate—five blunt pieces of glass, each notched and labeled in her sharp, impatient hand. Not pretty. Not delicate. Honest tools for stupid time.\n\nBram showed up from the branch corridor with Chloen at his shoulder, both of them wearing the soot-and-sweat look of workers who’d already dragged too many bodies out of too many wrong places.\n\nBram’s eyes hit the indicators first. Then Jhace’s hands.\n\n“You look like shit,” Bram said.\n\nJhace didn’t bother denying it. “You got your crew?”\n\n“Got whoever didn’t bolt upslope,” Bram said. “Foreman’s coming.”\n\nOf course he was.\n\nSector Fourteen had been treating Jhace like a delay cost for as long as he’d been crawling the bones. Now the city had punched back and made that foreman briefly human. Briefly. A pulse later, the man had remembered his favorite religion: output.\n\nA runner—Kylar, young, narrow, lungs still full of arrogance—skidded into the chamber and pointed at the branch corridor. “He’s here. And he brought Harlen.”\n\nJhace’s jaw tightened. Harlen meant paperwork with teeth. Harlen meant the kind of man who could turn a life-saving bleed into a disciplinary infraction with one clean sentence.\n\nTiffani stepped closer, voice low. “If they bury this, it dies.”\n\n“I know,” Jhace said. “That’s why we make it loud.”\n\nHe took the pin gauge and set it to the collar seam again. Same lie. Same hair-gap. Same wrong seat.\n\nThe hum under his palm wasn’t just wrong anymore. It was layered. The doubled deepening resonance sat beneath the normal cadence like a second spine.\n\nNotch two on the nearest indicator ghosted faintly.\n\nTiffani saw it and didn’t flinch. “Window’s tightening.”\n\nJhace nodded once. “Then we don’t wait.”\n\nHarlen arrived first—tall, clean coat, ink smell clinging to him like guilt. The Sector Fourteen foreman came behind, face tight, eyes restless, trying to pretend he’d never heard stone scream.\n\nHarlen’s gaze moved from Jhace to the indicators, then to Tiffani. “Explain why I was pulled from an active terrace.”\n\nTiffani didn’t speak. Not yet. She let Jhace handle the first strike. They’d decided that together: he spoke iron, she spoke glass. Let them hear both.\n\nJhace held up the pin gauge. “This collar is mis-seated. Junction Twelve. Sector Fourteen third branch feed. It walked during the last pulse. It will shear during the next if pressure holds.”\n\nHarlen’s eyes narrowed. “According to what standard?”\n\nJhace tapped the seam with the mallet—controlled. The tone that came back was wrong in a way even a clerk could hear. Too dull. Too thick. Holding breath.\n\n“According to the line,” Jhace said. “According to the sound and the seating and the movement.”\n\nThe foreman snorted. “You’re doing it again. Making ghosts out of vibration.”\n\nTiffani lifted one indicator and slid it across the stone toward Harlen. “Hold that. Don’t talk. Just hold it.”\n\nHarlen hesitated like the glass might accuse him. Then he took it between finger and thumb.\n\nA pulse crawled through the tunnel—small, clean, tunnel-wide.\n\nNotch two ghosted hard.\n\nHarlen’s face changed. Not fear. Not yet. Just the shock of being forced to feel something he hadn’t approved.\n\nThe foreman took a step back. “That’s… that’s a trick.”\n\nTiffani’s eyes went cold. “It’s glass. It doesn’t do tricks. It does stress.”\n\nJhace leaned toward the collar again. The metallic taste rose on his tongue, faint but unmistakable.\n\n“Notch two ghosting twice means pulse imminent,” Tiffani said, voice crisp. “Notch three means clear the corridor or bleed immediately.”\n\nHarlen looked at her slate. “Who authorized this protocol?”\n\n“Saint Merro,” Tiffani said. “It authorized it by trying to break.”\n\nHarlen’s mouth tightened. “That’s not an answer.”\n\nJhace stepped in. “Here’s the answer you want: bleed the branch now. Enough to matter. Or you’ll be doing a report over bodies you can’t unbreak.”\n\nThe foreman’s face reddened. “Kiln-quarter is live. We bleed too much and we lose—”\n\nAnother pulse hit, heavier than the crawl, snapping the lamps and shaking dust from the braces in a single synchronized beat.\n\nThe collar seam ticked.\n\nEveryone heard it.\n\nHarlen’s hand clenched around the indicator. Notch three ghosted—just a hair, just a warning trying to become a verdict.\n\nTiffani didn’t raise her voice. She didn’t need to. “That’s your last polite cue.”\n\nBram swore under his breath. Chloen shifted her stance like she was ready to sprint.\n\nThe foreman stared at Harlen. Harlen stared at the seam. Then, finally, Harlen nodded once like he hated the shape of his own decision.\n\n“Controlled bleed,” Harlen said. “Documented. Minimal disruption.”\n\nJhace shook his head. “Not minimal.”\n\nHarlen’s eyes flashed. “Inspector—”\n\nJhace pointed at the indicator still ghosting in Harlen’s grip. “That glass doesn’t give a damn about your disruption. Bleed enough to take load off the collar. Or don’t bleed at all and let it shear clean.”\n\nSilence, thick and ugly.\n\nHarlen made a choice that would haunt him later. “Enough,” he said, clipped. “Do it.”\n\nThe foreman’s jaw worked like he was chewing nails. He jerked his chin at two workers. “Valves. Now.”\n\nThe crew moved. Fast, for once. They ran the sequence Tiffani had drafted in hard, simple language. Call phrases. Hand signs. No room for bravado.\n\nJhace stayed at the collar, palm on iron, eyes on the seam. He watched the hum shift as pressure hissed down the branch.\n\nThe doubled resonance eased a fraction. The metallic taste thinned.\n\nNotch three cleared.\n\nThe collar didn’t move.\n\nA win, but not a victory.\n\nKylar reappeared, breath ragged, face gray. “Sector Nine—brace line failed. Took a man’s leg.”\n\nThe foreman flinched like he’d been slapped. Harlen’s eyes went sharp.\n\nTiffani’s mouth went tight. She didn’t look away from the indicator. She didn’t let grief steal her hands.\n\nBram muttered, “They’re gonna pin that on us.”\n\nJhace felt something hard settle in his chest.\n\n“That failure was coming whether we bled or not,” Jhace said. “But they’ll use it.”\n\nHarlen turned on him. “Keep your voice down.”\n\n“No,” Tiffani snapped, and now she did raise it—just enough to cut through the chamber. “We’re not whispering while the city breaks. We’re not letting you launder this into ‘settling’ again.”\n\nHarlen stared at her like she’d slapped him too. “Watch your tone.”\n\nTiffani stepped closer, indicator in hand, and pushed it toward him until he couldn’t ignore it. “Watch the glass.”\n\nThe hum deepened again, faintly. Notch two ghosted.\n\nJhace exhaled through his teeth. The pulses weren’t done. They weren’t “after.” They were now. Recurring. Shaping the city into something new whether anyone signed off or not.\n\nHe leaned toward Harlen, low and brutal. “You want a clean story. We want a living city. Pick.”\n\nHarlen’s gaze flicked to the corridor where the injured man’s cries echoed faintly. Then back to the collar that had just been saved. Then back to the indicator that wouldn’t stop telling on the world.\n\n“Run your protocol,” Harlen said, voice tight. “But if this turns into panic—”\n\n“It turns into survival,” Tiffani cut in. “Or it turns into graves.”\n\nJhace nodded once. Not grateful. Not hopeful. Just committed.\n\nThe city didn’t care who won arguments.\n\nIt cared who held.\n\n---\n\n## CHAPTER 6 — GLASS THAT TELLS  \n**Tide 2 | Shaping**  \n**POV: Tiffani**\n\nTiffani’s hands still remembered the furnace even when the city tried to make her forget it.\n\nThey remembered how heat softened pride. How glass punished impatience. How form only held when you respected stress instead of pretending it wasn’t there.\n\nNow she had to teach that discipline to people who didn’t care about glass.\n\nPeople who cared about not dying under it.\n\nThe glass-house had become a production line for survival tools—molds laid out like weapons, notch legends scratched into slate, indicator blanks stacked in rows like teeth.\n\nPella, broad-shouldered and blunt as a hammer, stood at the bench filing notch marks into a batch of cooling pieces. “These look like shit,” she said, approving.\n\n“They’re supposed to,” Tiffani replied. “Pretty gets you killed.”\n\nPella snorted. “Good. Because if a client asks for one of these, I’m hitting them.”\n\nTiffani almost smiled, then a pulse crawled through the floor and stole the humor out of the room.\n\nThe furnace breath hiccupped—heat surging, then dipping like a lung stuttering. The indicator blanks on the rack chimed softly as vibration passed through their cooling bodies.\n\nNot music. Not pretty. Just stress announcing itself.\n\nTiffani grabbed a fresh indicator—thick, blunt, readable—and held it near the doorway light. Notch one stayed clear. Notch two ghosted faintly.\n\nShe felt the metallic taste creep up, like memory trying to become prediction.\n\n“Batch’s reacting early,” Pella said, eyes narrowing. “That normal now?”\n\n“Nothing is normal,” Tiffani said. “We document it anyway.”\n\nShe scribbled in the anneal log: **Heat wobble + early ghosting. Treat notch two as notch three if it repeats.**\n\nShe hated how that sentence looked. It was messy. It wasn’t elegant. It was still true.\n\nBram showed up at the door with soot in his beard and tired in his eyes. Chloen behind him, carrying a coil of thin chain and a roll of seal wax like they were groceries.\n\nJhace stayed out of sight, which meant he was either in the tunnels or trying not to collapse where anyone could witness it.\n\nBram nodded at the racks. “How many you got?”\n\n“Enough to get people started,” Tiffani said. “Not enough to cover every branch.”\n\nChloen laid the chain on the bench. “Then you make enough. Because I’m not dragging another body out without warning.”\n\nTiffani’s throat tightened. “I’m trying.”\n\nPella slapped a finished indicator into a cloth wrap. “Stop trying. Start making.”\n\nTiffani held her temper tight. Rage didn’t improve annealing.\n\nShe pulled a gather, pressed it into a mold, turned it with practiced control. The glass resisted slightly—heat wobble again. She adjusted. She listened with her hands.\n\nNotch marks went in. Thickness held. She set it into the anneal box and marked it.\n\nAnother pulse rolled through, stronger. The whole shop shuddered like it was built on a living spine.\n\nNotch two on the nearest finished indicator ghosted twice, quick.\n\nTiffani’s stomach dropped.\n\n“Downslope window,” Bram said, already moving. “Jhace needs more.”\n\n“I know,” she snapped. “I know.”\n\nShe grabbed a batch—ten pieces, wrapped and tagged—and shoved them into a satchel.\n\nThen she stopped.\n\nBecause the indicators weren’t just reacting. They were inconsistent. Some ghosted early. Some stayed stubbornly clear until the last instant. If she sent these out raw, someone would trust the wrong one at the wrong time.\n\nShe needed calibration.\n\nNot theory. Not paperwork. Calibration in the bones where the pulses lived.\n\nTiffani dropped to a knee, pressed her palm flat to the stone floor, and did the thing she’d been trying not to rely on.\n\nSoulpulse wasn’t hers by craft. It was hers by necessity.\n\nShe didn’t shove it. She didn’t try to dominate it. She set it like a clamp and whispered the intent into her bones: **separate. read. compare.**\n\nThe world sharpened.\n\nNot bright. Not loud. Just… divided. Noise split into layers. Heat wobble became its own thread. Furnace breath became another. And beneath both, the pulse signature—clean, tunnel-born, the one that made iron taste bloom on her tongue.\n\nShe held it, longer than she should have.\n\nHer fingers trembled. Her forearm cramped. Nausea licked at the back of her throat.\n\nShe didn’t let go.\n\nShe moved her palm from stone to one indicator, then the next, using the pulse layer as a reference line. Some indicators tracked it clean. Some lagged. Some jumped early because of anneal variance.\n\nHer eyes watered. Her hand buzzed like it had been struck.\n\nPella crouched beside her. “You okay?”\n\n“No,” Tiffani whispered. “I’m useful.”\n\nShe forced her hand to keep moving—stone, indicator, stone, indicator—until she’d sorted the batch into three piles.\n\n“Good,” she said, voice tight. “Borderline. Scrap.”\n\nBram stared at the piles. “You just… felt that?”\n\nTiffani swallowed bile. “Don’t make it sound magical. It’s expensive.”\n\nChloen’s gaze softened, but she didn’t pity. “What do we do with borderline?”\n\nTiffani’s hand shook harder now. She flexed her fingers, trying to make them obey. “Borderline gets a second rule. If it ghosts once, treat it as warning. If it ghosts twice without clearing, treat it as immediate.”\n\nShe scribbled the revision into the protocol slate—simple language, worker-first, no approval stamps.\n\nBram read it, lips moving. “This’ll piss Harlen off.”\n\n“Good,” Tiffani said. “Harlen isn’t the one standing under braces.”\n\nThe Soulpulse cost hit in full a moment later—tremor biting her hand, nausea rolling in a thick wave, vision narrowing until the edges of the shop blurred.\n\nShe sat back hard against the bench leg, breathing through iron taste and sweat.\n\nPella slid a cup of water to her. “Drink.”\n\nTiffani drank. It tasted like metal anyway.\n\nBram took the calibrated pile—good indicators only—and tucked them into his pack. “Jhace needs these. Now.”\n\nTiffani nodded, wiping her mouth with the back of her wrist. “Tell him the protocol is updated. Borderline rule. Verification step.”\n\nChloen lifted the slate. “I’ll take a copy to the branch leads.”\n\nTiffani watched them leave, her body shaking, her stomach still trying to crawl up her throat.\n\nThat was one extended use. One expensive clamp.\n\nAnd it had turned her indicators from “proof” into “system.”\n\nShe leaned her head against the bench for a breath and told herself a hard truth:\n\nIf she kept using Soulpulse like this, it would eventually take something she couldn’t replace.\n\nBut if she didn’t, Saint Merro would take everything anyway.\n\n---\n\n## CHAPTER 7 — THE FIRST PROTOCOL RUN  \n**Tide 2 | Striking**  \n**POV: Jhace**\n\nProtocol didn’t mean safety.\n\nIt meant fewer surprises.\n\nJhace stood at a minor branch junction—Sector Eleven feed, not glamorous, not political—watching a crew of tired workers hold Tiffani’s indicator pieces like they were holy.\n\nHe hated that.\n\nTools weren’t holy. Tools were blunt truths that failed if you treated them like prayers.\n\nBram paced in the background, restless, while Chloen ran the checklist aloud in that firm voice that made men stop bullshitting.\n\n“Indicator state?” Chloen called.\n\nA worker held up his piece. “Notch one clear. Notch two faint ghost.”\n\n“Verification step,” Chloen snapped.\n\nGood. The word mattered now.\n\nJhace leaned in, set the pin gauge to the brace seat, checked the collar seam, ran fingers along the bracket pins. He ignored the tremor in his hands and trusted the sequence. Trust the method. Not your ego.\n\n“Pin fatigue on brace three,” he said.\n\nThe worker blinked. “What? It’s not cracked.”\n\n“It doesn’t have to crack to kill you,” Jhace said. “It just has to let go at the wrong time.”\n\nBram exhaled. “So it’s a false alarm?”\n\nJhace shook his head. “It’s a true warning for a different failure.”\n\nThis was the danger. The protocol could light up for the wrong reason. If it cried wolf once, management would bury it. If it missed a real failure, workers would die.\n\nJhace glanced at the indicator again. Notch two ghosted, cleared, ghosted.\n\nBorderline behavior.\n\nTiffani’s updated rule echoed in his head: ghost twice without clearing means immediate.\n\nHe watched. It ghosted, cleared.\n\nNot immediate. Not yet.\n\n“Replace the pin,” Jhace ordered. “Now.”\n\nA worker hesitated. “We don’t have—”\n\nChloen shoved a replacement pin into his hand. “We do. Move.”\n\nThey worked fast—pin puller biting, old pin squealing as it came free. Jhace kept his lamp steady, called out hand placements, made sure nobody put their face where the brace would whip if it decided to betray them.\n\nThe hum deepened under their boots.\n\nNotch two ghosted again.\n\nThis time it didn’t clear.\n\nChloen’s eyes snapped up. “Again.”\n\nThe indicator ghosted a second time without clearing.\n\nJhace felt the metallic taste bloom. The doubled resonance thickened in his teeth.\n\n“Clear corridor!” he barked. “Now!”\n\nNo arguing. No laughter. They’d seen enough.\n\nThe crew yanked tools back, fell behind stone cover, hands on bracing points.\n\nThe pulse hit a breath later—clean and heavy—ripping through the junction like a fist through cloth.\n\nThe new pin held.\n\nThe brace didn’t walk. The collar seam didn’t tick. Dust fell, lamps jittered, and nobody died.\n\nWhen the vibration eased, a worker let out a ragged laugh that sounded like someone trying to believe in something again.\n\nBram clapped Jhace’s shoulder. “That’s it. That’s the win.”\n\nJhace didn’t smile. He stared at the brace line like it was a predator that had decided to wait.\n\n“It’s a win,” he agreed. “But now comes the worse part.”\n\nChloen frowned. “What worse part?”\n\nJhace tilted his head upslope, where the administration lived and breathed ink. “They’re going to want to own it.”\n\nAs if summoned by the thought, Kylar came running in, eyes wide. “Harlen’s calling for you. Surface. Now. Says protocol is causing ‘unrest.’”\n\nJhace’s hands curled into fists. Tremor or not, he could still fight.\n\nHe looked at Chloen. “Keep drilling the checklist. Don’t let anyone skip verification because they’re tired.”\n\nChloen nodded once. “Got it.”\n\nHe looked at Bram. “If they try to twist this into ‘normal maintenance,’ you say no. Loud.”\n\nBram grinned without humor. “Oh, I’m gonna say no.”\n\nJhace took one last look at the brace that had held. Not because it deserved respect, but because it had proven something.\n\nThe method worked.\n\nNow he had to keep it alive in a world that preferred comforting lies.\n\n---\n\n## CHAPTER 8 — KILN-QUARTER PRESSURE  \n**Tide 3 | Quench**  \n**POV: Jhace**\n\nSector Fourteen smelled like heat and ego.\n\nThe kiln-quarter feed corridor ran hotter than anything else in the bones—constant pressure, constant demand, constant threat. Men worked here like the line would forgive them if they were fast enough.\n\nIt wouldn’t.\n\nJhace stepped into the staging area with Tiffani at his side and a crew gathered in a tight knot, eyes flicking between indicators and the foreman’s face like they were choosing which god to obey.\n\nHarlen stood near the map board, clean coat already stained with dust now. The Sector Fourteen foreman was there too, jaw tight, hands clenched behind his back like he was trying not to strangle someone.\n\n“Inspector,” Harlen said. “We keep the feed running. Controlled operations only.”\n\nJhace didn’t answer Harlen. He looked at the foreman.\n\n“You push us through a borderline window,” Jhace said, “and you’ll be signing your name on a shear.”\n\nThe foreman’s eyes flashed. “You don’t get to talk to me like—”\n\nTiffani cut in, voice sharp as notch marks. “He does, if you want your kiln-quarter intact.”\n\nThe foreman’s face reddened. “This is my sector.”\n\n“Then act like it,” Tiffani snapped. “Stop worshipping output and start worshipping stability.”\n\nA worker near the back—Salee, small, quick, eyes too old—held up her indicator. “Notch two’s ghosting already.”\n\nThe foreman scoffed. “It’s always ghosting now. You people see patterns in dust.”\n\nJhace took the indicator from Salee’s hand and watched it closely. The ghosting wasn’t random. It was rhythmic.\n\nGhost. Clear. Ghost. Clear.\n\nBorderline behavior.\n\nHe tasted iron faintly.\n\n“Sequence up,” Jhace ordered, ignoring the foreman’s glare. “We run the checklist. Verification step every time. No skipping because you’re scared or impatient.”\n\nThey moved into positions—valves, braces, collars—each person anchored where the protocol said, not where their pride wanted.\n\nTiffani stood at the map board with a slate, calling states as indicators shifted, eyes scanning like she was reading weather in glass.\n\n“Notch two ghost,” she called. “Clear. Ghost again. Watch for repeat.”\n\nJhace checked the collar seat at the main branch feed, pin gauge biting just right. Better than Junction Twelve, but still under too much load for comfort.\n\nA pulse crawled through the stone—small.\n\nNotch two ghosted across three indicators at once.\n\nTiffani’s eyes snapped to Jhace. “Window’s tightening.”\n\nThe foreman barked, “We proceed. Now. We can’t—”\n\nNotch two ghosted again without clearing.\n\nTiffani’s voice went hard. “Immediate.”\n\nJhace didn’t hesitate. “Bleed.”\n\nThe foreman lunged forward. “No! You bleed that line and I—”\n\nJhace shoved him back with a forearm, not gentle, not polite. “You’ll what? Fire me after you bury your workers?”\n\nThe foreman stumbled, shocked more by being touched than by the truth.\n\nThe valve crew moved. Controlled sequence. Hands placed where they wouldn’t get ripped off if the line jumped. Pressure hissed down the bleed channel, hot and angry.\n\nThen the corridor trembled.\n\nNot a crawl. A hit.\n\nThe pulse arrived early—stacked, twisted, nastier than the last.\n\nThe collar seam Jhace had just checked ticked—micro-movement, betrayal trying to become disaster.\n\nJhace’s hands were already shaking. Mechanical correction alone wouldn’t be fast enough.\n\nHe made the decision he hated.\n\nSoulpulse.\n\nHe planted his palm against the collar bracket and drove the intent in like a clamp: **hold the seat. stop the walk.**\n\nThe world sharpened into layers. He felt the collar’s micro-slip like teeth grinding. He felt the brace line’s load path. He felt the pulse trying to twist the joint out of truth.\n\nHe held.\n\nCost bit immediately—tremor turning violent, nausea slamming, vision narrowing until the corridor became a tunnel inside his skull. Metallic taste flooded his mouth so thick it felt like blood.\n\nHe held anyway.\n\n“Tighten!” he croaked.\n\nBram’s voice cut through, shouting at the valve crew. “Finish the bleed! Now!”\n\nA worker—Harlen’s assistant, some kid with too-clean hands—panicked and stepped into the wrong space, too close to the brace line.\n\nJhace saw it in the sharpened world: the brace was about to whip.\n\nHe couldn’t do both.\n\nHe couldn’t hold the collar and save the idiot.\n\nTriage wasn’t theory. It was a knife.\n\n“DOWN!” Jhace screamed, and threw his shoulder into the kid, knocking him behind the stone lip without releasing the collar.\n\nThe brace line snapped a fraction, scraping stone, showering sparks. The kid screamed—hurt, but alive.\n\nSomeone else wasn’t as lucky.\n\nA worker at the far end took the brace impact on the leg—bone cracking sound swallowed by the pulse roar. A howl filled the corridor, raw and animal.\n\nJhace’s hold broke as the pressure finally dropped enough for the collar to settle back into place without walking.\n\nHe staggered, hands shaking so hard he couldn’t close them, bile rising. He swallowed it down and tasted iron anyway.\n\nThe pulse faded.\n\nThe corridor was still.\n\nThen the screams came back in.\n\nHarlen rushed toward the injured worker, face pale now. The foreman stood frozen, eyes wide, watching the aftermath of his beloved output.\n\nTiffani’s hands were steady as she pressed cloth to the worker’s wound, voice low, commanding. “Hold pressure. Don’t let it open. Keep him breathing.”\n\nJhace leaned against the stone, shaking, and felt the cost of Soulpulse chewing through his nerves like a file.\n\nBram crouched beside him. “You saved the feed,” he said, voice rough. “You saved most of us.”\n\n“Not all,” Jhace rasped, staring at the blood on stone.\n\nTiffani looked up, eyes hard, grief contained in a vise. “This is what ‘minimal disruption’ buys,” she said, loud enough for Harlen and the foreman to hear. “It buys this.”\n\nHarlen’s jaw worked. He wanted to argue. He couldn’t. The corridor was still vibrating with proof.\n\nThe foreman finally found his voice, thin and ugly. “You did this. Your bleed—your—”\n\nTiffani stepped toward him like a storm contained in skin. “No. Your pressure did. Your refusal did. You want someone to blame? Blame the part of you that heard stone scream and still asked for output.”\n\nThe foreman’s face contorted. For a moment, he looked like a man.\n\nThen he looked away.\n\nKylar appeared in the corridor mouth, eyes wide. “Another branch is flipping early. Sector Twelve. Indicators are—”\n\nTiffani grabbed her satchel. “We move.”\n\nJhace tried to push off the wall. His legs wobbled. His hands shook like they belonged to someone else.\n\nHe forced them to obey anyway.\n\nBecause the city didn’t stop pulsing just because you paid a cost.\n\nAnd holding didn’t mean winning.\n\nIt meant showing up again.\n\n---"

            "## CHAPTER 1 — SOMETHING WRONG  \n**Tide 0 | Kindling**  \n**POV: Jhace**\n\nThe heat-grid hummed like it wanted to be trusted.\n\nNot loud. Not dramatic. Just that steady bone-deep push that made the Underface feel alive—iron breathing through stone, pressure riding the lines, the city’s weight sitting on every brace like a fat hand that never lifted.\n\nJhace set his palm flat to the main run and let the cadence climb into his wrist.\n\nOne. Two. Three.\n\nHe didn’t count out loud. You didn’t need a mouth for this. The grid spoke through callus.\n\nOne. Two. Three.\n\nGood. The cadence held. No chatter. No sharpness. No stutter. He slid downslope a span, then another, boots scuffing grit off the stone lip. The tunnel smelled like wet rock and old iron, with a sour thread of kiln-quarter heat bleeding in from Sector Fourteen.\n\nOne. Two—  \nThere.\n\nNot a break. Not a crack. A wrongness. A faint high rattle stitched into the hum, like someone trying to hide a lie under honest work.\n\nJhace pressed harder. The pipe warmed his palm through glove leather, steady as always, but the vibration under it didn’t match. It wasn’t the normal tremor from flow and heat. This was sharper. Thin. Denied by anyone who didn’t live with their hand on iron.\n\nHe pulled his measuring strap, marked the spot with chalk, and kept moving. Nobody respected a man who stopped a feed for “a feeling,” and Sector Fourteen didn’t respect anything that slowed kiln-quarter. They’d rather gamble a brace than lose a pour.\n\nThe tunnel narrowed at the next bend. Old stone here—older than any foreman’s name. Brace pins flashed dull in his lamp light, married to rock with repairs stacked on repairs. Saint Merro didn’t rebuild. Saint Merro patched and prayed.\n\nJhace’s boots crossed the seam where old run met newer graft. The hum shifted—normal at first—then the rattle returned, tighter now, as if he’d stepped into the mouth of the problem.\n\nHe found Junction Twelve by smell before sight: seal wax, old sweat, iron that had been reheated too often. The chamber opened around him, a knot of lines and brackets and braces, the city’s veins crossing like a drunk’s stitches.\n\nHe touched the collar.\n\nWarm. Slightly too warm. Not heat—pressure. The collar sat where it should, neat enough to fool a lazy eye. The tag read clean. The seal looked intact.\n\nJhace didn’t trust tags. He trusted seating.\n\nHe shifted his lamp angle and watched the shadow at the collar’s lip. A hairline gap—nothing most would call out. The kind of “nothing” that turned into a shear when the city decided to cough.\n\nHe set the pin gauge to the seam.\n\nIt didn’t bite right.\n\n“Fuck.” The word came out soft, more breath than voice.\n\nHe tested again, from a different angle. Same. The collar was mis-seated—barely. The kind of mis-seat that held until it didn’t, and when it didn’t, it took everything downslope with it like a snapped tendon.\n\nJhace marked it on the slate and felt the rattle sharpen under his palm, like the line agreed with him.\n\nVoices echoed from the branch corridor—boots and impatience. He didn’t look up. He didn’t need to. Sector Fourteen wore its authority like a hammer.\n\n“Jhace!” a man barked, the sound throwing itself off stone. “You stoppin’ again?”\n\nJhace stepped into the corridor and met them there. Three workers, one foreman—broad shoulders, clean gloves, the kind of clean that meant he didn’t seat collars himself. The foreman’s lamp was brighter than Jhace’s. His eyes weren’t.\n\n“This collar’s off,” Jhace said, keeping his voice even. “Junction Twelve. Third branch feed into your sector. It’s not seated.”\n\nThe foreman laughed like that was cute. “It’s seated. Tag says seated. You got your little strap marks and your little chalk like a priest. Doesn’t change the flow.”\n\nJhace held up the pin gauge. “Gauge says it’s off.”\n\n“Gauge says you like hearing yourself talk,” the foreman snapped. “Kiln-quarter is running. We’re not bleeding pressure because you got spooked by a hum.”\n\nJhace felt his jaw lock. He didn’t move away from the line. He kept his palm on iron, because the grid didn’t lie and men did.\n\n“It’s not local settling,” Jhace said. “You feel that rattle? That isn’t normal flow.”\n\nThe foreman’s eyes narrowed. “We don’t run by your feelings. We run by output.”\n\nBehind him, one of the workers shifted his stance, glancing at the collar like he wanted to believe Jhace but didn’t want to pay the price of belief.\n\nJhace tapped the seam with the mallet—light, controlled. The tone that came back wasn’t right. Too dull. Too thick. Like the collar was holding its breath.\n\nHe took a slow inhale.\n\nMetal. On his tongue.\n\nHe hated that taste. It was never here on a normal Tide. Metallic taste meant pressure doing things it wasn’t supposed to do.\n\n“You taste that?” Jhace asked, low.\n\nThe foreman sneered. “You want us to taste your ghosts now?”\n\nJhace slid his hand off the collar and pressed it to the brace beside it. The hum under the brace wasn’t just a hum. It was layered—like the usual cadence had grown a second shadow underneath.\n\nA doubled deepening, felt more than heard.\n\nHis teeth ached.\n\n“There’s a second register,” Jhace said. “Hear it? Not hear—feel it.”\n\nOne of the workers swallowed. “I… I feel someth—”\n\n“Shut up,” the foreman cut in. “If I hear one more—”\n\nThe tunnel pulsed.\n\nNot a little shiver. Not a local rock sigh. The entire chamber took a single unified beat, like the city’s spine had been struck with a hammer.\n\nDust fell off braces in the same instant. The lamps jittered. The hum dipped, then surged.\n\nJhace froze—not from fear, but from focus. That pulse traveled through the junction like it belonged to the line. Settling didn’t do that. Settling died in the stone. This… carried.\n\nHis tongue flooded with that metallic taste again, stronger. The doubled deepening resonance sharpened into a clean second layer, a dark under-hum that made his bones feel too small for his skin.\n\nThe workers went pale.\n\nThe foreman stared at the collar like he was seeing it for the first time.\n\n“That,” Jhace said, voice flat, “is not your output.”\n\nThe foreman’s throat bobbed. “It was… it was just—”\n\nAnother pulse rolled in behind the first, smaller but cleaner, like an echo that knew where to land. The collar seam ticked—just a tiny movement, a betrayal.\n\nJhace didn’t wait for permission.\n\n“Back,” he ordered the workers. “Give me space.”\n\nThe foreman lunged a step forward, rage trying to reclaim him. “You touch that valve and I’ll have you—”\n\nJhace’s eyes didn’t leave the seam. “You can have me after you have your dead.”\n\nHe jammed the wedge into place beneath the bracket, not as a fix, not as salvation—just a brace against the next beat. He set the mallet, struck once, twice. The collar held. Barely.\n\nHis hands were steady. His mind wasn’t. The wrongness in the line wasn’t just at Junction Twelve anymore. The whole tunnel carried it. The pulse had proven that.\n\nIf it was the whole tunnel, it was the whole city.\n\nThe foreman’s voice went thin. “We can’t shut down. Kiln-quarter—”\n\n“Then bleed,” Jhace said. “Controlled. Or you’ll get a bleed you don’t get to choose.”\n\nThe foreman glared, then spat to the side like that would fix physics. “Fine. Minimal. A sliver. You happy?”\n\nJhace shook his head. “Not minimal. Enough to matter.”\n\nThe foreman’s face reddened. “You don’t get to—”\n\nJhace turned, lamp light carving his expression hard. “You already heard the city hit you. You want to argue with the next one, do it with your face on the seam.”\n\nSilence.\n\nThen the foreman jerked his chin at a worker. “Do it. Quick.”\n\nJhace stayed by the collar while they moved. He listened through his palm. He watched the seam. He tasted iron and hate and fear.\n\nWhen the pressure hissed down the branch, the hum eased a fraction. Not fixed. Just less loaded.\n\nJhace marked the time on his slate in the only units that mattered: pulse count. The taste. The doubled resonance.\n\nHe turned away from Junction Twelve and started toward the ladder shaft.\n\nUpslope meant reports. Upslope meant arguments. Upslope meant people who’d try to sand the truth into something comfortable.\n\nDeeper meant origin. Deeper meant understanding what that pulse really was.\n\nAt the ladder shaft, iron rungs climbed into cooler air like a promise that didn’t pay out. Jhace put his boot on the first rung, then paused, hand still buzzing from the line.\n\nAnother faint pulse crawled through the stone—subtle, but tunnel-wide. Not local. Not settling.\n\nHe stared down into the dark run.\n\n“Deeper first,” he muttered. “Then up.”\n\nAnd he climbed.\n\n---\n\n## CHAPTER 2 — THE MEASURE OF A SKY  \n**Tide 0 | Kindling**  \n**POV: Tiffani**\n\nGlass told the truth even when people didn’t.\n\nIt told it the hard way—through stress lines and inclusions and the cruel honesty of fracture. It didn’t care about foremen or pride. It cared about heat, form, and the weight you forced it to hold.\n\nTiffani wiped her hands on her apron and watched the light at the glass-house doorway warp.\n\nNot much. A hair. Like the air had a bruise.\n\nShe stepped closer, squinting through a simple sighting frame she’d built from scrap rod and a bent bracket—nothing official, nothing sanctioned. Official instruments belonged to people who’d never had to make certainty out of scraps.\n\nThe warp drifted again, same direction, same angle, too consistent to be a trick of tired eyes.\n\nShe set the frame on the bench, pinned it with a weight, and took three readings—each one a mark on slate, each one a refusal to be dismissed as “nerves.”\n\nOutside, the terrace market muttered. Rumors ran faster than water. Somebody had heard a sky-scream from far terraces. Somebody’s cousin said the horizon flashed copper-gold.\n\nTiffani didn’t chase rumors. She chased repeatable.\n\nShe crossed the shop, heat licking her knuckles as she passed the furnace mouth. The furnace breathed steady, but even that breath felt… wrong. Not weaker. Not stronger. Misaligned. Like the rhythm was holding back a beat it didn’t know how to place.\n\nShe pulled a thin strip of cooled glass from a rack—clear, clean, annealed right—and held it near the doorway light. The strip was supposed to be dead quiet. No stress. No pattern.\n\nA faint ghost line shivered inside it.\n\nTiffani’s throat tightened.\n\nThat line wasn’t from her work. That line was from the air.\n\nShe laid the strip down and grabbed her slate. If she walked this to an office and said, “The light is wrong,” they’d pat her head and tell her to go pour pretty things.\n\nIf she walked it in with numbers, they’d ask who approved her numbers.\n\nIf she walked in with something that changed in their hands, they’d have to touch the truth.\n\nShe drew a fast grid. Not pretty. Functional. Two sighting marks, a baseline, a drift angle, a rough path. She didn’t pretend it was perfect. She just refused to pretend it was nothing.\n\nHer marks suggested the same thing every time she recalculated: whatever was happening wasn’t “falling” into Saint Merro.\n\nIt was passing through.\n\nEnter-side elsewhere. Exit-side consequences here.\n\nThat meant Saint Merro wouldn’t see the object first. Saint Merro would feel the aftermath like a fist through stone.\n\nShe swallowed, tasting dust.\n\n“No,” she whispered, because whispering didn’t change math.\n\nShe wrapped the clear strip in cloth and shoved it into her satchel beside calipers and a small fracture loupe. Then she added a second piece—thicker, blunt, with notch marks filed along the edge. If she was right, stress would show there first.\n\nShe hesitated, hand hovering over a small jar of copper-gold particulate—a vanity pigment some clients liked. She left it. This wasn’t decoration. This was witness.\n\nTiffani stepped out onto the terrace and headed for the administrative threshold.\n\nA clerk sat behind a stone lip with ink-stained fingers and a face carved from procedure.\n\nTiffani set her slate down. “I need this read by someone who can order a shutdown.”\n\nThe clerk glanced at it like it smelled. “For what cause?”\n\n“For what’s coming through the sky,” Tiffani said.\n\nThe clerk’s eyes flicked up, bored and already dismissing. “We don’t process ‘sky.’ We process damages and requests.”\n\n“It’s not a request,” she snapped, then forced herself to breathe. Rage didn’t pass gates. Proof did. “It’s measurement.”\n\nHe tapped the slate with a finger. “Who certified this instrument?”\n\n“My hands,” she said. “Same hands that keep glass from exploding in your face.”\n\nHis mouth twitched like he might laugh. “That’s not a certification.”\n\nTiffani leaned in. “Then certify this: if you delay, you’ll be certifying bodies.”\n\nHe looked past her, already reaching for the next petition. “Bring an approved report. Or take your concerns to a foreman.”\n\nShe stood there for a breath too long, waiting for him to become a person.\n\nHe didn’t.\n\nFine.\n\nIf the gate wanted approved, she’d make approval irrelevant.\n\nShe went back to her shop, heart hammering, and stoked the furnace harder. Heat rose. The glass-house filled with that bright throat-burn warmth that made sweat bead at the hairline.\n\nShe pulled a gather on her rod—thick, stubborn, glowing. She needed a piece that would hold stress like a ledger, a piece that would crack in a readable way when the air did whatever it was about to do.\n\nShe set the molten gather into a simple mold with notch marks. Pressed. Turned. Lifted.\n\nThe glass took shape—ugly, blunt, honest.\n\nAs she worked it, that faint warp at the doorway thickened. Light bent like it didn’t trust the world’s edges anymore.\n\nThe witness glass in her hand twitched—just a tiny internal shiver, a stress signature forming without any cause from her flame.\n\nTiffani’s breath caught.\n\nIt’s early, she thought. It’s already touching us.\n\nShe set the piece into the anneal box, slammed the lid, and scribbled on her slate: **If notch three ghosts, treat as high risk.**\n\nHer hands didn’t shake. Her stomach did.\n\nShe packed three witness pieces into cloth, added her rough path grid, and stepped out onto the terrace again.\n\nThis time she didn’t go to gates.\n\nShe went downslope.\n\nToward the only kind of person who’d believe a hum and a taste and a pulse without asking who stamped it.\n\nToward the Underface.\n\n---\n\n## CHAPTER 3 — PIERCING: STREET-LEVEL  \n**Tide 0 | Pouring**  \n**POV: Jhace**\n\nThe first scream came from stone, not mouths.\n\nJhace had just cleared the ladder shaft into street air when the terrace beneath his boots lurched. Not a settling sway. A violent, unified shove—like the entire city had been struck from below.\n\nPeople staggered. A cart toppled. Glass in a shopfront spidered in a single breath and then burst outward, raining knives.\n\nJhace ducked, throwing his forearm over his head. A shard skimmed his sleeve and bit skin. He didn’t feel it. The air tasted like iron filings.\n\nA second beat rolled through—cleaner, deeper. The sound arrived late, a delayed roar that made the market stalls rattle and the terrace stones complain.\n\nSomebody yelled, “Down! Down!”\n\nSomebody else yelled a name like that name could stop physics.\n\nJhace’s head snapped toward the skyline—no fireball, no falling rock, no obvious threat. Just the light bending wrong, like the sky itself had been dented.\n\nThat was worse.\n\nA woman near a fountain slipped, caught herself on the lip, and stared at her hand like it didn’t belong to her. Blood ran between her fingers—thin and bright.\n\n“Move!” Jhace barked, grabbing a man by the shoulder and yanking him out of the street as a masonry bracket tore loose from an upper terrace and fell. It hit the stones where the man had stood, splitting rock like bread.\n\nPanic poured through bodies like spilled oil.\n\nJhace forced his eyes to do what they always did: ignore the noise, find the load. Where does it fail first? What’s carrying too much?\n\nHe saw it—an overhead brace line that shouldn’t be moving, vibrating with that doubled deepening resonance he’d felt below. It wasn’t just shaking. It was answering the pulse, amplifying it, like a tuning fork hammered by the world.\n\nA child stood under it, frozen, mouth open and silent.\n\nJhace didn’t think. He moved.\n\nHe sprinted, boots slipping on dust, and shoved the child sideways as the brace pin above them screamed and began to walk out of its seat.\n\nThe pin didn’t pop free yet. It just crept—slow, inevitable.\n\nJhace’s hands hit the stone support and the iron brace at the same time.\n\nThe world sharpened.\n\nSoulpulse wasn’t a spell. It was a decision to spend yourself.\n\nHe drove the intent into his palm—hold. Just hold. Not forever. Not heroic. Enough.\n\nThe doubled resonance became layers he could separate. The brace line’s stress became visible as pressure in his bones. The pin’s seat became a map of micro-slips.\n\nHis muscles locked. His teeth clenched.\n\nThe brace stopped walking for a handful of breaths.\n\nCost punched him immediately—nausea rising like hot bile, tremor trying to invade his forearms, vision tunneling at the edges. He didn’t let go.\n\n“GET AWAY FROM THE BRACE!” he shouted, voice tearing.\n\nPeople scattered. Some listened. Some didn’t.\n\nA second pulse hit—stronger, nastier, shaped differently. Not just a shove. A twist.\n\nJhace’s hold shuddered. The pin jumped a fraction. His stomach heaved. The metallic taste flooded his mouth so hard it felt like he’d bitten a coin.\n\nThe hold broke.\n\nThe brace snapped free with a sound like the city ripping cloth.\n\nJhace threw himself forward and dragged the child under the nearest stone arch, rolling with him as the brace line whipped down and gouged the street, carving sparks out of stone.\n\nHe landed hard. Shoulder pain flared. His hands shook like they weren’t his.\n\nPeople screamed. A stall caught fire from a toppled heat-lamp. Smoke rose. Above, an upper terrace wall cracked in a clean line, then began to shed stone in chunks.\n\nSaint Merro was being asked to carry a load it hadn’t agreed to.\n\nJhace pushed up, trembling. He tried to breathe through the nausea. Every breath tasted like iron.\n\nHe saw the Underface access grate across the street—the mouth back into the bones. The pulses were traveling through everything. Street, stone, pipe, brace. The whole city was one instrument now.\n\nHe had to get back down. Not to hide. To stabilize.\n\nAnother pulse rolled in, and the entire street dipped—just enough to send people falling into each other.\n\nJhace stumbled toward the grate.\n\nThen he saw her.\n\nA woman moving against the panic flow, downslope, satchel tight to her side, face focused in a way that didn’t belong to terror. She wasn’t running. She was measuring.\n\nTiffani.\n\nHe didn’t know her name yet. He knew the type: hands that did exact work. Eyes that didn’t lie to themselves.\n\nShe met his gaze for one breath—just long enough for him to see she’d felt the same wrongness and decided it mattered.\n\nAnother pulse hit.\n\nJhace’s legs threatened to fold. He had to spend again or he’d be useless.\n\nHe slapped his palm to the terrace stone beside the grate and drove Soulpulse into it—not to hold a brace this time, but to read. To parse the new shape of the pulse.\n\nThe world sharpened in a different way. The pulse had a rhythm now. Not random. A sequence. A stacking pattern that made certain structures amplify and others dampen.\n\nIt wasn’t “earthquake.” It was feedback.\n\nHe ripped his hand away, gasping, cost clawing through him—tremor now full, hands buzzing, vision flickering. He could barely close his fingers.\n\nBut he’d learned one thing worth the blood:\n\nThe next pulse would hit harder in the Underface, where the grid carried it like a throat carries a shout.\n\nHe looked at Tiffani and croaked, “Down. Now.”\n\nShe didn’t argue. She just moved.\n\nAnd together they dropped into the city’s bones as Saint Merro screamed above them.\n\n---\n\n## CHAPTER 4 — ASH IN THE FLOW  \n**Tide 1 | Shaping**  \n**POV: Tiffani**\n\nAfter the first terror, the city tried to pretend.\n\nIt swept shards into piles. It propped fallen braces with bad lumber. It called injuries “incidents” and cracks “settling.” It told itself a story where the worst was over, because admitting the worst was beginning meant admitting nobody was in charge.\n\nTiffani didn’t have the luxury of pretending.\n\nHer shop smelled like smoke and cold ash. The furnace still breathed, but its rhythm wasn’t steady anymore. Heat came in uneven waves, as if the fuel itself was confused by the new pulses.\n\nShe laid her witness glass on the bench and watched stress lines crawl inside it like trapped lightning.\n\nNot from her hand. From the air.\n\nFrom the grid beneath the stone.\n\nShe had gotten downslope at the right time. Not because she was brave, but because she was done being ignored. She’d found the tunnel man—Jhace—half-trembling, eyes too sharp, mouth too tight, like he’d bitten down on the truth hard enough to crack teeth.\n\nHe’d told her very little. He didn’t need to. The Underface had said the rest through her soles.\n\nNow she needed a repeatable signal. Something that could be taught. Something that didn’t require one man’s palm or her own stubbornness.\n\nShe picked up a thick indicator piece—blunt, notched, designed for stupid time, when people’s brains turned to smoke and only hands remembered.\n\nNotch one: baseline.  \nNotch two: warning.  \nNotch three: get the fuck out or bleed it now.\n\nShe watched notch two ghost. Then clear. Then ghost again.\n\nA pulse was coming.\n\nShe didn’t have a clock for it. She had the hum. The taste. The way the furnace breath hit her chest.\n\nShe tried to steady her hands and did what she’d never admitted out loud she could do.\n\nSoulpulse wasn’t just in tunnels. It wasn’t just for men who lived under stone. It was a tool some people carried in their blood like an inherited bruise.\n\nTiffani placed her palm on the indicator glass and pushed the intent into it—read. Not hold. Not force. Just read the stress before it became fracture.\n\nNothing.\n\nNo sharpening. No clarity. Just her own heartbeat in her fingers and the glass sitting there like dead sand.\n\nHer stomach dropped.\n\nShe tried again, harder, anger rising with the push—read, damn you—\n\nPain lanced up her arm. Not heat. Not burn. A deep internal wrongness, like she’d twisted a joint against its seat. Nausea surged. Her vision blurred at the edges.\n\nThe Soulpulse failed, and it punished her for trying.\n\nShe yanked her hand back and leaned over the bench, swallowing bile. Her fingers trembled—fine tremor, not Jhace’s violent shake, but enough to ruin a pour.\n\n“Fine,” she rasped. “Fine. I don’t need you.”\n\nShe forced herself upright. Took a breath. Then another. Let the panic burn out into focus.\n\nSoulpulse wasn’t a hammer. It was a clamp. A controlled pressure. You didn’t throw it. You set it.\n\nShe tried again, smaller. Narrower. One intention only: *separate noise from signal.*\n\nHer palm touched the glass.\n\nThe world sharpened by a hair. Not dramatic. Not cinematic. Just enough.\n\nStress lines inside the indicator resolved into layers—some from her anneal, some from the shop’s temperature drift, and one set that pulsed in time with the hum under the floor.\n\nThere.\n\nThat one wasn’t hers.\n\nShe held it for a breath too long and paid for it—tremor biting her fingertips, eyes watering, mouth filling with that metallic taste like she’d licked a coin.\n\nBut she got what she needed.\n\nShe snatched her slate and scribbled, hand shaking: **When notch two ghosts twice without clearing, pulse imminent. When notch three ghosts, bleed pressure or clear the corridor.**\n\nShe didn’t dress it up. She didn’t soften it. Workers didn’t need poetry. They needed survival language.\n\nShe gathered every thick indicator she’d made and wrapped them in cloth. She added a simple legend card: notch states, action steps. She grabbed her calipers and a fracture loupe and her anneal log, because if this became system, she needed records that outlived her.\n\nOutside, the terrace wind carried ash and the distant sound of people trying to rebuild lies into shelter.\n\nTiffani headed downslope again.\n\nNot because she wanted to live under stone, but because the stone was where the truth traveled first.\n\nAt the Underface access, guards tried to stop her. She didn’t argue. She held out an indicator piece and watched it ghost under their noses as the hum deepened.\n\nThey stared. One of them swallowed, face pale.\n\n“Let me through,” she said. “Or you can stand here and pretend until the stone eats you.”\n\nThey moved.\n\nDown below, the Underface breathed warm iron and wet rock. The hum was never quiet now. It layered itself like a warning that had learned how to speak.\n\nJhace stood near a junction map board, hands still trembling, eyes hollowed by cost. He looked up when he heard her boots.\n\n“You’re back,” he said, voice rough.\n\n“I’m not leaving,” she answered, and set the wrapped indicators on the stone lip like offerings to a god that didn’t deserve them. “This is a threshold system. Readable. Repeatable.”\n\nHe stared at the notch marks, then at her slate.\n\n“You used it,” he said, and it wasn’t a question.\n\nShe didn’t lie. “Tried. Failed. Tried again. Got enough.”\n\nJhace’s mouth tightened—respect or fear, she couldn’t tell. “It’ll cost you.”\n\n“It already did,” she said, flexing trembling fingers. “But now it costs *less* than bodies.”\n\nA faint pulse crawled through the tunnel—small, but clean.\n\nNot local.\n\nTunnel-wide.\n\nJhace’s eyes snapped to the indicators. So did hers.\n\nNotch two ghosted.\n\nTwice.\n\nHe looked at her slate, then at the branch corridor.\n\n“Bleed,” he ordered, voice carrying. “Controlled. Now.”\n\nWorkers moved—fast this time. No arguing. No laughing.\n\nTiffani watched them go and felt something shift in her chest—not relief, not victory. Commitment. The ugly kind. The kind that meant your life stopped being yours.\n\nJhace leaned close, voice low. “If we’re doing this, we do it right. No soft words. No approval stamps.”\n\nTiffani met his gaze. “Good. Because I’m done asking permission.”\n\nAnother pulse came, heavier.\n\nThe indicators ghosted in unison.\n\nAnd the Underface began to run their new method like it had always existed—because the alternative was Saint Merro breaking itself into graves.\n\n--- \n\nIf you want, I can continue with **Ch5–Ch8** next in the same style and constraints, and I’ll keep the updated canon hooks tight (Tide 0–3 compression, 7 Soulpulse uses total, 25-beat pacing feel without listing beats)."

            "import os, textwrap, zipfile, json, yaml, datetime, pathlib, re\n\nbase_dir = \"/mnt/data/N01_GripThatHolds_InnocentBible\"\nif os.path.exists(base_dir):\n    # clean\n    for root, dirs, files in os.walk(base_dir, topdown=False):\n        for f in files:\n            os.remove(os.path.join(root, f))\n        for d in dirs:\n            os.rmdir(os.path.join(root, d))\n    os.rmdir(base_dir)\n\nos.makedirs(base_dir, exist_ok=True)\n\ndef write(path, content):\n    full = os.path.join(base_dir, path)\n    os.makedirs(os.path.dirname(full), exist_ok=True)\n    with open(full, \"w\", encoding=\"utf-8\") as f:\n        f.write(content)\n\nreadme = \"\"\"# N01: THE GRIP THAT HOLDS — Innocent Exponential Story Bible (Repo Export)\n\n**Scope lock:** N01 only. No meta-layer.  \n**Project:** Te’Oga / Saint Merro / Underface / Piercing (exit-side effects)  \n**Format:** Markdown-first + YAML data for tooling.\n\n## Folder map\n- `00_SCOPE/` — scope constraints, logline, theme, promise\n- `01_WORLD/` — Saint Merro + Underface, infrastructure behaviors\n- `02_SYSTEMS/` — Soulpulse (N01 rules), craft-as-plot rules\n- `03_CHARACTERS/` — Jhace, Tiffani, relationship track, opposition\n- `04_STRUCTURE/` — 12-chapter grid + continuity checklist\n- `05_CHAPTER_PACKETS/` — chapter-by-chapter “exponential” packets (scene lists)\n- `data/` — machine-readable YAML + JSON exports\n\n## How to use\n1) Start with `00_SCOPE/SCOPE_LOCK.md` (what you may/should NOT add).  \n2) Read `04_STRUCTURE/CHAPTER_GRID.md` for the spine.  \n3) Generate prose chapter-by-chapter using `05_CHAPTER_PACKETS/CH##_*.md` as the only source.  \n4) If you build tools, ingest `data/n01_bible.yaml`.\n\n## Canon anchors (non-negotiable in N01)\n- Chapter 1 is **pre-Piercing**. cannot solve Piercing; buys seconds and data.\n\n### Usage discipline\n- Early: 0 uses until earned.\n- First use: emergency perception/hold only.\n- Later: used sparingly, always with consequence.\n\n## Craft-as-Plot\n### Smithing / infrastructure (Jhace)\n- Torque, seating, collars, braces, load paths, creep, shear, slip.\n- Fix actions must: reveal data OR prevent harm OR raise Build chapter packets: 3–6 scenes per chapter with required fields.\ndef chapter_packet(ch_num, title, pov, tide_phase, scenes, soulpulse_budget=\"0 uses\"):\n    md = [f\"# CH{ch_num:02d} — {title}\",\n          \"\",\n          f\"**POV:** {pov}\",\n          f\"**Time:** {tide_phase}\",\n          f\"**Soulpulse budget:** {soulpulse_budget}\",\n          \"\",\n          \"## Chapter objective\",\n          scenes.get(\"chapter_objective\",\"\"),\n          \"\",\n          \"## Chapter constraints\",\n          \"\\n\".join([f\"- {c}\" for c in scenes.get(\"constraints\",[])]),\n          \"\",\n          \"## Scene list (beat-by-beat)\",\n          \"\"]\n    for i, sc in enumerate(scenes[\"scene_list\"], start=1):\n        md.extend([\n            f\"### Scene {i}: {sc['name']}\",\n            f\"- **Location:** {sc['location']}\",\n            f\"- **Objective:** {sc['objective']}\",\n            f\"- **Obstacles:** \" + \"; \".join(sc.get(\"obstacles\",[])),\n            f\"- **Props/tools:** \" + \", \".join(sc.get(\"props\",[])),\n            f\"- **Sensory palette:** \" + \"; \".join(sc.get(\"sensory\",[])),\n            f\"- **Decision point:** {sc['decision']}\",\n            f\"- **Fail-state if wrong:** {sc['fail_state']}\",\n            f\"- **Exit hook:** {sc['hook']}\",\n            \"\"\n        ])\n    md.extend([\n        \"## End-state (must be true when chapter ends)\",\n        \"\\n\".join([f\"- {e}\" for e in scenes.get(\"end_state\",[])]),\n        \"\"\n    ])\n    return \"\\n\".join(md)\n\n# Define scenes per
